<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Magnesium</h1>			<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">

				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a> <i>(Mn)</i>.</div>

</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">chemical element with atomic number 12</div><table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><caption><span style="white-space:nowrap">Magnesium,&#160;&#160;<sub><span style="font-size:smaller;">12</span></sub>Mg</span></caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">

<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;m&#39; in &#39;my&#39;">m</span><span title="/æ/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;bad&#39;">æ</span><span title="/ɡ/: &#39;g&#39; in &#39;guy&#39;">ɡ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="/iː/: &#39;ee&#39; in &#39;fleece&#39;">iː</span><span title="&#39;z&#39; in &#39;zoom&#39;">z</span><span title="/i/: &#39;y&#39; in &#39;happy&#39;">i</span><span title="/ə/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">ə</span><span title="&#39;m&#39; in &#39;my&#39;">m</span></span>/</a></span></span>&#x20;<wbr />&#8203;<span class="nowrap">(<a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling">mag-<span style="font-size:90%">NEE</span>-zee-əm</i></a>)</span></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Appearance</th><td>

<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_magnesium" title="Template:Infobox magnesium"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_magnesium" title="Template talk:Infobox magnesium"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_magnesium&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div>&#x20;|&#x20;<a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Magnesium</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with symbol&#160;<b>Mg</b> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a>&#160;12. It is a shiny gray solid which bears a close physical resemblance to the other five elements in the second column (group 2, or <a href="/wiki/Alkaline_earth_metals" class="mw-redirect" title="Alkaline earth metals">alkaline earth metals</a>) of the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a>: all group 2 elements have the same <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electron</a> configuration in the outer electron shell and a similar crystal structure.
</p><p>Magnesium is the ninth most abundant element in the universe.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> It is produced in large, aging <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a> from the sequential addition of three <a href="/wiki/Helium_nucleus" class="mw-redirect" title="Helium nucleus">helium nuclei</a> to a <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> nucleus. When such stars explode as <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernovas</a>, much of the magnesium is expelled into the <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a> where it may recycle into new star systems. Magnesium is the eighth most abundant element in the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_crust" class="mw-redirect" title="Earth&#39;s crust">Earth's crust</a><sup id="cite_ref-Abundance_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abundance-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> and the fourth most common element in the Earth (after <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a>), making up 13% of the planet's mass and a large fraction of the planet's <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a>. It is the third most abundant element dissolved in seawater, after <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Magnesium occurs naturally only in combination with other elements, where it invariably has a +2 <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation state</a>. The free element (metal) can be produced artificially, and is highly reactive (though in the atmosphere, it is soon coated in a thin layer of oxide that partly inhibits reactivity – see <a href="/wiki/Passivation_(chemistry)" title="Passivation (chemistry)">passivation</a>). The free metal burns with a characteristic brilliant-white light. The metal is now obtained mainly by <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a> of magnesium <a href="/wiki/Salt_(chemistry)" title="Salt (chemistry)">salts</a> obtained from <a href="/wiki/Brine" title="Brine">brine</a>, and is used primarily as a component in <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a>-magnesium alloys, sometimes called <i><a href="/wiki/Magnalium" title="Magnalium">magnalium</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Magnalium" title="Magnalium">magnelium</a></i>. Magnesium is less dense than <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a>, and the alloy is prized for its combination of lightness and strength.
</p><p>Magnesium is the eleventh most abundant element by mass in the <a href="/wiki/Human_body" title="Human body">human body</a> and is essential to all cells and some 300 <a href="/wiki/Enzymes" class="mw-redirect" title="Enzymes">enzymes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nih_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nih-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Magnesium ions interact with <a href="/wiki/Polyphosphate" title="Polyphosphate">polyphosphate</a> compounds such as <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">ATP</a>, <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a>, and <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a>. Hundreds of enzymes require magnesium ions to function.  Magnesium compounds are used medicinally as common <a href="/wiki/Laxatives" class="mw-redirect" title="Laxatives">laxatives</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antacids" class="mw-redirect" title="Antacids">antacids</a> (e.g., milk of magnesia), and to stabilize abnormal nerve excitation or blood vessel spasm in such conditions as <a href="/wiki/Eclampsia" title="Eclampsia">eclampsia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nih_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nih-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_properties">Physical properties</span></h3>
<p>Elemental magnesium is a gray-white lightweight metal, two-thirds the density of aluminium. It <a href="/wiki/Tarnish" title="Tarnish">tarnishes</a> slightly when exposed to air, although, unlike the other <a href="/wiki/Alkaline_earth_metals" class="mw-redirect" title="Alkaline earth metals">alkaline earth metals</a>, an oxygen-free environment is unnecessary for storage because magnesium is protected by a thin layer of oxide that is fairly impermeable and difficult to remove. Magnesium has the lowest melting (923&#160;K (1,202&#160;°F)) and the lowest boiling point 1,363&#160;K (1,994&#160;°F) of all the alkaline earth metals.
</p><p>Magnesium reacts with water at room temperature, though it reacts much more slowly than calcium, a similar group 2 metal.  When submerged in water, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> bubbles form slowly on the surface of the metal – though, if powdered, it reacts much more rapidly. The reaction occurs faster with higher temperatures (see <a href="/wiki/Magnesium#Safety_precautions" title="Magnesium">safety precautions</a>). Magnesium's reversible reaction with water can be harnessed to store energy and run a <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_injection_cycle" title="Magnesium injection cycle">magnesium-based engine</a>.
</p><p>Magnesium also reacts exothermically with most acids such as <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a> (HCl), producing the metal chloride and hydrogen gas, similar to the HCl reaction with aluminium, zinc, and many other metals.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_properties">Chemical properties</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Flammability">Flammability</span></h4>
<p>Magnesium is highly <a href="/wiki/Flammability" class="mw-redirect" title="Flammability">flammable</a>, especially when powdered or shaved into thin strips, though it is difficult to ignite in mass or bulk. Flame temperatures of magnesium and magnesium alloys can reach 3,100&#160;°C (5,610&#160;°F),<sup id="cite_ref-Dreizin,_Edward_L.;_Berman,_Charles_H._and_Vicenzi,_Edward_P._2000_30_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dreizin,_Edward_L.;_Berman,_Charles_H._and_Vicenzi,_Edward_P._2000_30-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> although flame height above the burning metal is usually less than 300&#160;mm (12&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-DOE_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DOE-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>  Once ignited, such fires are difficult to extinguish, because combustion continues in <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> (forming <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_nitride" title="Magnesium nitride">magnesium nitride</a>), <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> (forming <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_oxide" title="Magnesium oxide">magnesium oxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>), and water (forming magnesium oxide and hydrogen). This property was used in incendiary weapons during the <a href="/wiki/Firebombing" title="Firebombing">firebombing</a> of cities in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, where the only practical <a href="/wiki/Civil_defense" title="Civil defense">civil defense</a> was to smother a burning flare under dry sand to exclude atmosphere from the combustion.
</p><p>Magnesium may also be used as an igniter for <a href="/wiki/Thermite" title="Thermite">thermite</a>, a mixture of aluminium and iron oxide powder that ignites only at a very high temperature.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Source_of_light">Source of light</span></h4>
<p>When burning in air, magnesium produces a brilliant-white light that includes strong ultraviolet wavelengths. Magnesium powder (<a href="/wiki/Flash_powder" title="Flash powder">flash powder</a>) was used for subject illumination in the early days of <a href="/wiki/Photography" title="Photography">photography</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Later, magnesium filament was used in electrically ignited single-use photography <a href="/wiki/Flash_(photography)#Flashbulbs" title="Flash (photography)">flashbulbs</a>. Magnesium powder is used in <a href="/wiki/Fireworks" title="Fireworks">fireworks</a> and marine <a href="/wiki/Flare" title="Flare">flares</a> where a brilliant white light is required.  It was also used for various theatrical effects,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> such as lightning,<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> pistol flashes,<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> and supernatural appearances.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Magnesium_minerals" title="Category:Magnesium minerals">Category:Magnesium minerals</a>.</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Boninite" title="Boninite">Boninite</a></div>
<p>Magnesium is the eighth-most-abundant element in the Earth's crust by mass and tied in seventh place with <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> in <a href="/wiki/Molarity" class="mw-redirect" title="Molarity">molarity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Abundance_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abundance-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> It is found in large deposits of <a href="/wiki/Magnesite" title="Magnesite">magnesite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dolomite" title="Dolomite">dolomite</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">minerals</a>, and in mineral waters, where magnesium ion is soluble.
</p><p>Although magnesium is found in more than 60 <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">minerals</a>, only <a href="/wiki/Dolomite" title="Dolomite">dolomite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnesite" title="Magnesite">magnesite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brucite" title="Brucite">brucite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carnallite" title="Carnallite">carnallite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Talc" title="Talc">talc</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Olivine" title="Olivine">olivine</a> are of commercial importance.
</p><p>The <span class="chemf nowrap">Mg<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Cation" class="mw-redirect" title="Cation">cation</a> is the second-most-abundant cation in seawater (about ⅛ the mass of sodium ions in a given sample), which makes seawater and sea salt attractive commercial sources for Mg. To extract the magnesium, <a href="/wiki/Calcium_hydroxide" title="Calcium hydroxide">calcium hydroxide</a> is added to <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">seawater</a> to form <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_hydroxide" title="Magnesium hydroxide">magnesium hydroxide</a> <a href="/wiki/Precipitate" class="mw-redirect" title="Precipitate">precipitate</a>.
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">MgCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">Ca(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">Mg(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">CaCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>Magnesium hydroxide (<a href="/wiki/Brucite" title="Brucite">brucite</a>) is insoluble in water and can be filtered out and reacted with <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a> to produced concentrated <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_chloride" title="Magnesium chloride">magnesium chloride</a>.
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">Mg(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> + 2 HCl → <span class="chemf nowrap">MgCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> + 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span></dd></dl>
<p>From magnesium chloride, <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a> produces magnesium.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Forms">Forms</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alloy">Alloy</span></h3>
<p>As of 2013, magnesium alloy consumption was less than one million tons per year, compared with 50 million tons of <a href="/wiki/Aluminum_alloy" class="mw-redirect" title="Aluminum alloy">aluminum alloys</a>. Its use has been historically limited by its tendency to corrode, <a href="/wiki/Creep_(deformation)" title="Creep (deformation)">creep</a> at high temperatures, and combust.<sup id="cite_ref-giz_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-giz-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Corrosion">Corrosion</span></h4>
<p>The presence of <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cobalt" title="Cobalt">cobalt</a> strongly activates <a href="/wiki/Corrosion" title="Corrosion">corrosion</a>. Greater than a very small percentage, these metals precipitate as <a href="/wiki/Intermetallic_compound" class="mw-redirect" title="Intermetallic compound">intermetallic compounds</a>, and the precipitate locales function as active <a href="/wiki/Cathode" title="Cathode">cathodic</a> sites that reduce water, causing the loss of magnesium.<sup id="cite_ref-giz_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-giz-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>  Controlling the quantity of these metals improves corrosion resistance. Sufficient <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a> overcomes the corrosive effects of iron. This requires precise control over composition, increasing costs.<sup id="cite_ref-giz_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-giz-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Adding a cathodic poison captures atomic hydrogen within the structure of a metal. This prevents the formation of free hydrogen gas, an essential factor of corrosive chemical processes. The addition of about one in three hundred parts <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a> reduces its corrosion rate in a salt solution by a factor of nearly ten.<sup id="cite_ref-giz_17-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-giz-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="High-temperature_creep_and_flammability">High-temperature creep and flammability</span></h4>
<p>Research showed that magnesium's tendency to creep at high-temperatures is eliminated by the addition of <a href="/wiki/Scandium" title="Scandium">scandium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gadolinium" title="Gadolinium">gadolinium</a>. Flammability is greatly reduced by a small amount of <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a> in the alloy.<sup id="cite_ref-giz_17-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-giz-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Compounds">Compounds</span></h3>
<p>Magnesium forms a variety of compounds important to industry and biology, including <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_carbonate" title="Magnesium carbonate">magnesium carbonate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_chloride" title="Magnesium chloride">magnesium chloride</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_citrate" title="Magnesium citrate">magnesium citrate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_hydroxide" title="Magnesium hydroxide">magnesium hydroxide</a> (milk of magnesia), <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_oxide" title="Magnesium oxide">magnesium oxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_sulfate" title="Magnesium sulfate">magnesium sulfate</a>, and magnesium sulfate heptahydrate (<a href="/wiki/Epsom_salts" class="mw-redirect" title="Epsom salts">Epsom salts</a>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_magnesium" title="Isotopes of magnesium">Isotopes of magnesium</a></div>
<p>Magnesium has three stable <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a>: <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">24</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Mg</span>, <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">25</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Mg</span> and <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">26</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Mg</span>. All are present in significant amounts (see table of isotopes above). About 79% of Mg is <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">24</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Mg</span>. The isotope <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">28</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Mg</span> is radioactive and in the 1950s to 1970s was produced by several nuclear power plants for use in scientific experiments. This isotope has a relatively short half-life (21 hours) and its use was limited by shipping times.
</p><p>The nuclide <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">26</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Mg</span> has found application in <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopic</a> <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">geology</a>, similar to that of aluminium. <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">26</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Mg</span> is a <a href="/wiki/Radiogenic" class="mw-redirect" title="Radiogenic">radiogenic</a> daughter product of <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">26</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Al</span>, which has a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of 717,000 years. Excessive quantities of stable <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">26</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Mg</span> have been observed in the <a href="/wiki/Ca-Al-rich_inclusions" class="mw-redirect" title="Ca-Al-rich inclusions">Ca-Al-rich inclusions</a> of some <a href="/wiki/Carbonaceous_chondrite" title="Carbonaceous chondrite">carbonaceous chondrite</a> <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorites</a>. This anomalous abundance is attributed to the decay of its parent <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">26</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Al</span> in the inclusions, and researchers conclude that such meteorites were formed in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_nebula" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar nebula">solar nebula</a> before the <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">26</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Al</span> had decayed.  These are among the oldest objects in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar system">solar system</a> and contain preserved information about its early history.
</p><p>It is conventional to plot <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">26</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Mg</span>/<span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">24</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Mg</span> against an Al/Mg ratio. In an <a href="/wiki/Isochron_dating" title="Isochron dating">isochron dating</a> plot, the Al/Mg ratio plotted is<span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">27</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Al</span>/<span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">24</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Mg</span>. The slope of the isochron has no age significance, but indicates the initial <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">26</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Al</span>/<span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">27</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Al</span> ratio in the sample at the time when the systems were separated from a common reservoir.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_magnesium_production" title="List of countries by magnesium production">List of countries by magnesium production</a></div>

</th></tr>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mg_sheets_and_ingots.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Mg_sheets_and_ingots.jpg/220px-Mg_sheets_and_ingots.jpg" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Mg_sheets_and_ingots.jpg/330px-Mg_sheets_and_ingots.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Mg_sheets_and_ingots.jpg/440px-Mg_sheets_and_ingots.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2448" data-file-height="1960" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mg_sheets_and_ingots.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Magnesium sheets and ingots</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="People&#39;s Republic of China">China</a> is the dominant supplier of magnesium, with approximately 80% of the world market share. China is almost completely reliant on the <a href="/wiki/Silicothermic_reaction" title="Silicothermic reaction">silicothermic</a> <a href="/wiki/Pidgeon_process" title="Pidgeon process">Pidgeon process</a> (the reduction of the oxide at high temperatures with silicon, often provided by a ferrosilicon alloy in which the iron is but a spectator in the reactions) to obtain the metal.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The process can also be carried out with <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> at approx 2300&#160;°C:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">2MgO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">(s)</sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">Si<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">(s)</sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">2CaO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">(s)</sub></span></span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">2Mg<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">(g)</sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">Ca<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>SiO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4(s)</sub></span></span></dd>
<dd><span class="chemf nowrap">MgO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">(s)</sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">(s)</sub></span></span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">Mg<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">(g)</sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">(g)</sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>In the United States, magnesium is obtained principally with the <a href="/wiki/Dow_process" title="Dow process">Dow process</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a> of fused magnesium chloride from <a href="/wiki/Brine" title="Brine">brine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sea_water" class="mw-redirect" title="Sea water">sea water</a>. A saline solution containing <span class="chemf nowrap">Mg<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> ions is first treated with <a href="/wiki/Calcium_oxide" title="Calcium oxide">lime</a> (calcium oxide) and the precipitated <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_hydroxide" title="Magnesium hydroxide">magnesium hydroxide</a> is collected:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">Mg<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">(aq)</sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">CaO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">(s)</sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">Ca<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">(aq)</sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">Mg(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2(s)</sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>The hydroxide is then converted to a partial <a href="/wiki/Hydrate" title="Hydrate">hydrate</a> of <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_chloride" title="Magnesium chloride">magnesium chloride</a> by treating the hydroxide with <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a> and heating of the product:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">Mg(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2(s)</sub></span></span> + 2 HCl → <span class="chemf nowrap">MgCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2(aq)</sub></span></span> + 2<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">(l)</sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>The salt is then electrolyzed in the molten state. At the <a href="/wiki/Cathode" title="Cathode">cathode</a>, the <span class="chemf nowrap">Mg<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> ion is reduced by two <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> to magnesium metal:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">Mg<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + 2 <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>e<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">&#8722;</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> → Mg</dd></dl>
<p>At the <a href="/wiki/Anode" title="Anode">anode</a>, each pair of <span class="chemf nowrap">Cl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> ions is oxidized to <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> gas, releasing two electrons to complete the circuit:
</p>
<dl><dd>2 <span class="chemf nowrap">Cl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">Cl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> (g) + 2 <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>e<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">&#8722;</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>A new process, solid oxide membrane technology, involves the electrolytic reduction of MgO. At the cathode, <span class="chemf nowrap">Mg<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> ion is reduced by two <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> to magnesium metal. The electrolyte is <a href="/wiki/Yttria-stabilized_zirconia" title="Yttria-stabilized zirconia">yttria-stabilized zirconia</a> (YSZ). The anode is a liquid metal. At the YSZ/liquid metal anode <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> is oxidized. A layer of graphite borders the liquid metal anode, and at this interface carbon and oxygen react to form carbon monoxide. When silver is used as the liquid metal anode, there is no reductant carbon or hydrogen needed, and only oxygen gas is evolved at the anode.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Use_of_Solid-Oxide-Membrane_Technology_for_Electrometallurgy_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Use_of_Solid-Oxide-Membrane_Technology_for_Electrometallurgy-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> It has been reported that this method provides a 40% reduction in cost per pound over the electrolytic reduction method.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> This method is more environmentally sound than others because there is much less carbon dioxide emitted.
</p><p>The United States has traditionally been the major world supplier of this metal, supplying 45% of world production even as recently as 1995. Today, the US market share is at 7%, with a single domestic producer left, US Magnesium, a <a href="/wiki/Renco_Group" title="Renco Group">Renco Group</a> company in <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a> born from now-defunct Magcorp.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bolton-davy.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Bolton-davy.jpg/160px-Bolton-davy.jpg" width="160" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Bolton-davy.jpg/240px-Bolton-davy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Bolton-davy.jpg/320px-Bolton-davy.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="1102" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bolton-davy.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">Humphry Davy</a></div></div></div>
<p>The name magnesium originates from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> word for a district in <a href="/wiki/Thessaly" title="Thessaly">Thessaly</a> called <a href="/wiki/Magnesia_(regional_unit)" title="Magnesia (regional unit)">Magnesia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> It is related to <a href="/wiki/Magnetite" title="Magnetite">magnetite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a>, which also originated from this area, and required differentiation as separate substances. See <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a> for this history.
</p><p>In 1618, a farmer at Epsom in England attempted to give his cows water from a well there. The cows refused to drink because of the water's bitter taste, but the farmer noticed that the water seemed to heal scratches and rashes. The substance became known as <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_sulfate" title="Magnesium sulfate">Epsom salts</a> and its fame spread.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> It was eventually recognized as hydrated magnesium sulfate, <span class="chemf nowrap">MgSO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>·7&#8202;<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>.
</p><p>The metal itself was first isolated by <a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">Sir Humphry Davy</a> in England in 1808. He used electrolysis on a mixture of magnesia and <a href="/wiki/Mercury(II)_oxide" title="Mercury(II) oxide">mercuric oxide</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Davy1808_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davy1808-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Bussy" title="Antoine Bussy">Antoine Bussy</a> prepared it in coherent form in 1831. Davy's first suggestion for a name was magnium,<sup id="cite_ref-Davy1808_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davy1808-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> but the name magnesium is now used.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses_as_a_metal">Uses as a metal</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-12062,_Wasserreiter_mit_Magnesiumfackeln.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-12062%2C_Wasserreiter_mit_Magnesiumfackeln.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-12062%2C_Wasserreiter_mit_Magnesiumfackeln.jpg" width="220" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-12062%2C_Wasserreiter_mit_Magnesiumfackeln.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-12062%2C_Wasserreiter_mit_Magnesiumfackeln.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-12062%2C_Wasserreiter_mit_Magnesiumfackeln.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-12062%2C_Wasserreiter_mit_Magnesiumfackeln.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="618" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-12062,_Wasserreiter_mit_Magnesiumfackeln.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An unusual application of magnesium as an <a href="/wiki/Illumination_(lighting)" class="mw-redirect" title="Illumination (lighting)">illumination</a> source while <a href="/wiki/Wakeskating" title="Wakeskating">wakeskating</a> in 1931</div></div></div>
<p>Magnesium is the third-most-commonly-used structural metal, following <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> and aluminium.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The main applications of magnesium are, in order: aluminium alloys, <a href="/wiki/Die-casting" class="mw-redirect" title="Die-casting">die-casting</a> (alloyed with <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-BakerM._M._Avedesian1999_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BakerM._M._Avedesian1999-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> removing <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> in the production of iron and steel, and the production of <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">titanium</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Kroll_process" title="Kroll process">Kroll process</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Magnesium is used in super-strong, lightweight materials and alloys.  For example, when infused with silicon carbide nanoparticles, it has extremely high specific strength.<sup id="cite_ref-UCLA_Research_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UCLA_Research-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Historically, magnesium was one of the main aerospace construction metals and was used for German military aircraft as early as World War I and extensively for German aircraft in World War II.
</p><p>The Germans coined the name "<a href="/wiki/Elektron_(alloy)" title="Elektron (alloy)">Elektron</a>" for magnesium alloy, a term which is still used today. In the commercial aerospace industry, magnesium was generally restricted to engine-related components, due to fire and corrosion hazards. Currently, magnesium alloy use in aerospace is increasing, driven by the importance of fuel economy.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Development and testing of new magnesium alloys continues, notably Elektron 21, which (in test) has proved suitable for aerospace engine, internal, and airframe components.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The European Community runs three R&amp;D magnesium projects in the Aerospace priority of Six Framework Program.
</p><p>In the form of thin ribbons, magnesium is used to <a href="/wiki/Solvent#Desiccant" title="Solvent">purify solvents</a>; for example, preparing super-dry ethanol.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aircraft">Aircraft</span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Wright_Aeronautical" title="Wright Aeronautical">Wright Aeronautical</a> used a magnesium <a href="/wiki/Crankcase" title="Crankcase">crankcase</a> in the WWII-era <a href="/wiki/Wright_R-3350" class="mw-redirect" title="Wright R-3350">Wright Duplex Cyclone</a> aviation engine. This presented a serious problem for the earliest models of the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_B-29" class="mw-redirect" title="Boeing B-29">Boeing B-29</a> heavy bomber when an in-flight engine fire ignited the engine crankcase.  The resulting combustion was as hot as 5,600&#160;°F (3,100&#160;°C) and could sever the wing spar from the <a href="/wiki/Fuselage" title="Fuselage">fuselage</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Automotive">Automotive</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mg_alloy_car_engine_blocks.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Mg_alloy_car_engine_blocks.jpg/220px-Mg_alloy_car_engine_blocks.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Mg_alloy_car_engine_blocks.jpg/330px-Mg_alloy_car_engine_blocks.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Mg_alloy_car_engine_blocks.jpg/440px-Mg_alloy_car_engine_blocks.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2657" data-file-height="1779" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mg_alloy_car_engine_blocks.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mg alloy motorcycle engine blocks</div></div></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Mercedes-Benz" title="Mercedes-Benz">Mercedes-Benz</a> used the alloy <a href="/wiki/Elektron_(alloy)" title="Elektron (alloy)">Elektron</a> in the body of an early model <a href="/wiki/Mercedes-Benz_300_SLR" title="Mercedes-Benz 300 SLR">Mercedes-Benz 300 SLR</a>; these cars ran (with successes) at <a href="/wiki/24_Hours_of_Le_Mans" title="24 Hours of Le Mans">Le Mans</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Mille_Miglia" title="Mille Miglia">Mille Miglia</a>, and other world-class race events in 1955.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Porsche" title="Porsche">Porsche</a> used magnesium alloy frames in the <a href="/wiki/Porsche_917" title="Porsche 917">917/053</a> that won Le Mans in 1971, and continues to use magnesium alloys for its engine blocks due to the weight advantage.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Volkswagen_Group" title="Volkswagen Group">Volkswagen Group</a> has used magnesium in its engine components for many years.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_Motors" title="Mitsubishi Motors">Mitsubishi Motors</a> uses magnesium for its <a href="/wiki/Paddle_shifters" class="mw-redirect" title="Paddle shifters">paddle shifters</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/BMW" title="BMW">BMW</a> used magnesium alloy blocks in their <a href="/wiki/BMW_N52" title="BMW N52">N52</a> engine, including an aluminium alloy insert for the cylinder walls and cooling jackets surrounded by a high-temperature magnesium alloy AJ62A.  The engine was used worldwide between 2005 and 2011 in various 1, 3, 5, 6, and 7 series models; as well as the Z4, X1, X3, and X5.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Chevrolet" title="Chevrolet">Chevrolet</a> used the magnesium alloy AE44 in the 2006 Corvette <a href="/wiki/Corvette_C6" class="mw-redirect" title="Corvette C6">Z06</a>.</li></ul>
<p>Both AJ62A and AE44 are recent developments in high-temperature low-<a href="/wiki/Creep_(deformation)" title="Creep (deformation)">creep</a> magnesium alloys. The general strategy for such alloys is to form <a href="/wiki/Intermetallic" title="Intermetallic">intermetallic</a> precipitates at the <a href="/wiki/Crystallite" title="Crystallite">grain boundaries</a>, for example by adding <a href="/wiki/Mischmetal" title="Mischmetal">mischmetal</a> or <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> New alloy development and lower costs that make magnesium competitive with aluminium will increase the number of automotive applications.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electronics">Electronics</span></h3>
<p>Because of low weight and good mechanical and electrical properties, magnesium is widely used for manufacturing of mobile phones, laptop and <a href="/wiki/Tablet_computers" class="mw-redirect" title="Tablet computers">tablet computers</a>, cameras, and other electronic components.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Magnesium-products.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Magnesium-products.jpg/220px-Magnesium-products.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Magnesium-products.jpg/330px-Magnesium-products.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Magnesium-products.jpg/440px-Magnesium-products.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Magnesium-products.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Products made of magnesium: firestarter and shavings, sharpener, magnesium ribbon</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span></h3>
<p>Magnesium, being readily available and relatively nontoxic, has a variety of uses:
</p>
<ul><li>Magnesium is flammable, burning at a temperature of approximately 3,100&#160;°C (3,370&#160;K; 5,610&#160;°F),<sup id="cite_ref-Dreizin,_Edward_L.;_Berman,_Charles_H._and_Vicenzi,_Edward_P._2000_30_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dreizin,_Edward_L.;_Berman,_Charles_H._and_Vicenzi,_Edward_P._2000_30-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Autoignition_temperature" title="Autoignition temperature">autoignition temperature</a> of magnesium ribbon is approximately 473&#160;°C (746&#160;K; 883&#160;°F).<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> It produces intense, bright, white light when it burns. Magnesium's high combustion temperature makes it a useful tool for starting emergency fires. Other uses include flash <a href="/wiki/Photography" title="Photography">photography</a>, flares, <a href="/wiki/Pyrotechnics" title="Pyrotechnics">pyrotechnics</a>, and fireworks sparklers. Magnesium is also often used to ignite thermite or other materials that require a high ignition temperature.<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Magnesium_Sparks.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Magnesium_Sparks.jpg/220px-Magnesium_Sparks.jpg" width="220" height="192" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Magnesium_Sparks.jpg/330px-Magnesium_Sparks.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0d/Magnesium_Sparks.jpg 2x" data-file-width="430" data-file-height="376" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Magnesium_Sparks.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Magnesium firestarter (in left hand), used with a <a href="/wiki/Pocket_knife" class="mw-redirect" title="Pocket knife">pocket knife</a> and flint to create sparks that ignite the shavings</div></div></div></li>
<li>In the form of turnings or ribbons, to prepare <a href="/wiki/Grignard_reagent" class="mw-redirect" title="Grignard reagent">Grignard reagents</a>, which are useful in <a href="/wiki/Organic_synthesis" title="Organic synthesis">organic synthesis</a>.</li>
<li>As an additive agent in conventional propellants and the production of <a href="/wiki/Ductile_iron" title="Ductile iron">nodular graphite in cast iron</a>.</li>
<li>As a reducing agent to separate <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a> and other metals from their <a href="/wiki/Salt_(chemistry)" title="Salt (chemistry)">salts</a>.</li>
<li>As a <a href="/wiki/Sacrificial_anode" class="mw-redirect" title="Sacrificial anode">sacrificial (galvanic) anode</a> to protect boats, underground tanks, pipelines, buried structures, and water heaters.</li>
<li>Alloyed with zinc to produce the zinc sheet used in <a href="/wiki/Photoengraving" title="Photoengraving">photoengraving</a> plates in the printing industry, <a href="/wiki/Dry_cell" title="Dry cell">dry-cell battery</a> walls, and <a href="/wiki/Flashing_(weatherproofing)" title="Flashing (weatherproofing)">roofing</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BakerM._M._Avedesian1999_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BakerM._M._Avedesian1999-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>As a metal, this element's principal use is as an alloying additive to aluminium with these aluminium-magnesium alloys being used mainly for <a href="/wiki/Beverage_can" class="mw-redirect" title="Beverage can">beverage cans</a>, sports equipment such as golf clubs, fishing reels, and archery bows and arrows.</li>
<li>Specialty, high-grade car wheels of magnesium alloy are called "<a href="/wiki/Magnesium_alloy_wheel" class="mw-redirect" title="Magnesium alloy wheel">mag wheels</a>", although the term is often misapplied to aluminium wheels. Many car and aircraft manufacturers have made engine and body parts from magnesium.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Magnesium_battery" title="Magnesium battery">Magnesium batteries</a> have been commercialized as <a href="/wiki/Primary_battery" class="mw-redirect" title="Primary battery">primary batteries</a>, and are an active topic of research for rechargeable <a href="/wiki/Secondary_battery" class="mw-redirect" title="Secondary battery">secondary batteries</a>.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety_precautions">Safety precautions</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div id="mwe_player_0" class="PopUpMediaTransform" style="width:220px;" videopayload="&lt;div class=&quot;mediaContainer&quot; style=&quot;width:480px&quot;&gt;&lt;video id=&quot;mwe_player_1&quot; data-height=&quot;360&quot; data-bandwidth=&quot;1520888&quot; data-framerate=&quot;25&quot;/&gt;&lt;source src=&quot;//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/d/db/Burning_Magnesium_Block%21.ogv/Burning_Magnesium_Block%21.ogv.120p.vp9.webm&quot; type=&quot;video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp9, opus&amp;quot;&quot; data-title=&quot;Low bandwidth VP9 (180P)&quot; data-shorttitle=&quot;VP9 180P&quot; data-transcodekey=&quot;180p.vp9.webm&quot; data-width=&quot;240&quot; data-height=&quot;180&quot; data-bandwidth=&quot;208864&quot; data-framerate=&quot;25&quot;/&gt;&lt;source src=&quot;//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/d/db/Burning_Magnesium_Block%21.ogv/Burning_Magnesium_Block%21.ogv.240p.vp9.webm&quot; type=&quot;video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp9, opus&amp;quot;&quot; data-title=&quot;Small VP9 (240P)&quot; data-shorttitle=&quot;VP9 240P&quot; data-transcodekey=&quot;240p.vp9.webm&quot; data-width=&quot;320&quot;
